     <p>The <b>University of Notre Dame du Lac</b> (or simply <b>Notre Dame</b> <span class="nowrap"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˌ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'n' in 'nigh'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">n</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/oʊ/ long 'o' in 'bode'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">oʊ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'t' in 'tie'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">t</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ər/ 'er' in 'finger'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ər</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˈ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'d' in 'dye'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">d</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/eɪ/ long 'a' in 'base'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">eɪ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'m' in 'my'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">m</span></a></span><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling" class="Unicode"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Wikipedia:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span class="SMALLCAPS" style="FONT-VARIANT:SMALL-CAPS;"><span class="NOCAPS" style="TEXT-TRANSFORM:LOWERCASE;">NOH</span></span></span></b>-tər-<b><span class="smallcaps"><span class="SMALLCAPS" style="FONT-VARIANT:SMALL-CAPS;"><span class="NOCAPS" style="TEXT-TRANSFORM:LOWERCASE;">DAYM</span></span></span></b></i></a></span>) is a Catholic <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">research university</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame,_Indiana" title="Notre Dame, Indiana">Notre Dame</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Unincorporated_area" title="Unincorporated area">unincorporated community</a> north of the city of <a href="/wiki/South_Bend,_Indiana" title="South Bend, Indiana">South Bend</a>, in <a href="/wiki/St._Joseph_County,_Indiana" title="St. Joseph County, Indiana">St. Joseph County, Indiana</a>, United States. &quot;Notre Dame,&quot; <small><a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> pronunciation:&nbsp;​</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_French" title="Help:IPA for French">[nɔtʁ dam]</a></span> meaning &quot;Our Lady,&quot; is a Catholic honorific salutation in reference to the <a href="/wiki/Blessed_Virgin_Mary_(Roman_Catholic)" title="Blessed Virgin Mary (Roman Catholic)">Virgin Mary</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">patron saint</a> of the university.</p> 
     <p>The school was founded by Father <a href="/wiki/Edward_Sorin" title="Edward Sorin">Edward Sorin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Congregation_of_Holy_Cross" title="Congregation of Holy Cross">CSC</a>, who was also its first president, and even today many Holy Cross priests serve the school—most notably the president of the university. It was established as an all-male institution on November 26, 1842, on land donated by the Bishop of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_Indianapolis" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Indianapolis">Vincennes</a>, Indiana. The university first enrolled women undergraduates in 1972. As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> about 47 percent of the student body was female.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Notre Dame's Catholic character is reflected in its explicit commitment to the Catholic faith, numerous ministries funded by the school, and the architecture around campus. In 1962, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> called it one of the Catholic Ivy Universities in American <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic" class="mw-redirect">Roman Catholic</a> higher education.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university today is organized into five colleges and one professional school, and its graduate program awards 32 master's and 25 doctoral degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Over 80% of the university's 8,000 undergraduates live on campus in one of 29 single-sex residence halls, each of which fields teams for more than a dozen <a href="/wiki/Intramural_sports" title="Intramural sports">intramural sports</a>, and the university counts approximately 120,000 alumni.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Notre Dame's athletic teams are members of the <a href="/wiki/Division_I_(NCAA)" title="Division I (NCAA)">NCAA Division I</a> and are known collectively as the <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_Fighting_Irish" title="Notre Dame Fighting Irish">Fighting Irish</a>. The football team, an <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I_FBS_independent_schools" title="NCAA Division I FBS independent schools">Independent</a>, has accumulated eleven consensus national championships, seven <a href="/wiki/Heisman_Trophy" title="Heisman Trophy">Heisman Trophy</a> winners, and 62 members in the <a href="/wiki/College_Football_Hall_of_Fame" title="College Football Hall of Fame">College Football Hall of Fame</a>. Other ND teams, chiefly in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Coast_Conference" title="Atlantic Coast Conference">Atlantic Coast Conference</a>, have accumulated 16 national championships.</p>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundations">Foundations</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Foundations">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>In 1842, the Bishop of Vincennes, <a href="/w/index.php?title=C%C3%A9lestine_Guynemer_de_la_Hailane&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="C&eacute;lestine Guynemer de la Hailane (page does not exist)">C&eacute;lestine Guynemer de la Hailane</a>, offered land to Father Edward Sorin of the Congregation of Holy Cross, on the condition that he build a college in two years.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Sorin arrived on the site with eight Holy Cross brothers on November 26, 1842, and began the school using Father <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Badin" title="Stephen Badin">Stephen Badin</a>'s old log chapel. They immediately acquired two students and set about building additions to the campus.</p> 
     <p>Notre Dame began as a primary and secondary school, but soon received its official college charter from the Indiana General Assembly on January 15, 1844.<sup id="cite_ref-Hope.2C_C.S.C._1979_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hope.2C_C.S.C._1979-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Under the charter the school is officially named the University of Notre Dame du Lac (University of Our Lady of the Lake).<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Because the university was originally only for male students, the female-only <a href="/wiki/Saint_Mary%27s_College_(Indiana)" title="Saint Mary's College (Indiana)">Saint Mary's College</a> was founded by the <a href="/wiki/Sisters_of_the_Holy_Cross" title="Sisters of the Holy Cross">Sisters of the Holy Cross</a> near Notre Dame in 1844.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>More students attended the college; the first degrees were awarded in 1849.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> The university was expanded with new buildings to accommodate more students and faculty.<sup id="cite_ref-Hope.2C_C.S.C._1979_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hope.2C_C.S.C._1979-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> With each new president, new academic programs were offered and new buildings built to accommodate them. The original Main Building built by Sorin just after he arrived was replaced by a larger &quot;Main Building&quot; in 1865, which housed the university's administration, classrooms, and dormitories. Beginning in 1873, a library collection was started by Father Lemonnier. By 1879 it had grown to ten thousand volumes that were housed in the Main Building.</p> 
     <p>This Main Building, and the library collection, was destroyed by a fire in April 1879, and rebuilt before the next school year.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> The library collection was also rebuilt and stayed housed in the new Main Building for years afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Around the time of the fire, a Music Hall was opened. Eventually becoming known as Washington Hall, it hosted plays and musical acts put on by the school.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:The_University_of_Notre_Dame_%22God_Quad%22.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/The_University_of_Notre_Dame_%22God_Quad%22.JPG/390px-The_University_of_Notre_Dame_%22God_Quad%22.JPG" width="390" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/The_University_of_Notre_Dame_%22God_Quad%22.JPG/585px-The_University_of_Notre_Dame_%22God_Quad%22.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/The_University_of_Notre_Dame_%22God_Quad%22.JPG/780px-The_University_of_Notre_Dame_%22God_Quad%22.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:The_University_of_Notre_Dame_%22God_Quad%22.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> A view of the University's historic center, the Main Quadrangle, popularly termed the God Quad.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>By 1880, a science program was established at the university, and a Science Hall was built in 1883. The hall housed multiple classrooms and science labs needed for early research at the university.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> By 1890, individual residence halls were built to house the increasing number of students.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>William J. Hoynes (1846–1919) was dean of the law school 1883-1919, and when its new building was opened shortly after his death it was renamed in his honor.<sup id="cite_ref-Marvin_R._O.27Connell_2001_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marvin_R._O.27Connell_2001-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/John_Augustine_Zahm" title="John Augustine Zahm">John Zahm</a> C.S.C. (1851–1921) became the Holy Cross Provincial for the United States (1896–1906), with overall supervision of the university, He tried to transform Notre Dame into a great university, erecting buildings and added to the campus art gallery and library, and amassing what became a famous Dante collection. His term was not renewed because of fears he had expanded Notre Dame too quickly and had run the Holy Cross order into serious debt.<sup id="cite_ref-Marvin_R._O.27Connell_2001_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marvin_R._O.27Connell_2001-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <ul class="gallery"></ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hesburgh_Era:_1952.E2.80.931987">Hesburgh Era: 1952–1987</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Hesburgh Era: 1952–1987">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Theodore_Hesburgh" title="Theodore Hesburgh">Theodore Hesburgh</a>, C.S.C., (born 1917) served as president for 35 years (1952–87) of dramatic transformations. In that time the annual operating budget rose by a factor of 18 from $9.7 million to $176.6 million, and the endowment by a factor of 40 from $9 million to $350 million, and research funding by a factor of 20 from $735,000 to $15 million. Enrollment nearly doubled from 4,979 to 9,600, faculty more than doubled 389 to 950, and degrees awarded annually doubled from 1,212 to 2,500.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Hesburgh is also credited with transforming the face of Notre Dame by making it a <a href="/wiki/Coeducation" title="Coeducation" class="mw-redirect">coeducational</a> institution. In the mid-1960s Notre Dame and Saint Mary's College developed a co-exchange program whereby several hundred students took classes not offered at their home institution, an arrangement that added undergraduate women to a campus that already had a few women in the graduate schools. Nearly a third of accepted Notre Dame students chose not to enroll because of its single-sex status,<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and a 1968 poll indicated that nearly three-fourths of all Notre Dame students considered transferring to a coeducational school. After extensive debate, merging with St. Mary's was rejected, primarily because of the differential in faculty qualifications and pay scales. &quot;In American college education,&quot; explained Rev. Charles E. Sheedy, C.S.C., Notre Dame's Dean of Arts and Letters, &quot;certain features formerly considered advantageous and enviable are now seen as anachronistic and out of place.... In this environment of diversity, the integration of the sexes is a normal and expected aspect, replacing separatism.&quot; Thomas Blantz, C.S.C., Notre Dame's Vice President of Student Affairs, added that coeducation &quot;opened up a whole other pool of very bright students.&quot;[<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Two of the male residence halls were converted for the newly admitted female students that first year,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> while two others were converted for the next school year.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> The first female student, a transfer from St. Mary's College, graduated in 1972 with a bachelor's degree in marketing.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Malloy_Era:_1987.E2.80.932005">Malloy Era: 1987–2005</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Malloy Era: 1987–2005">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>In 18 years under President <a href="/wiki/Edward_Malloy" title="Edward Malloy">Edward Malloy</a>, C.S.C., (1987–2005), there was a rapid growth in the school's reputation, faculty, and resources. He increased the faculty by more than 500 professors; the academic quality of the student body has improved dramatically, the average SAT score rose from 1240 to 1360; the number of minority students more than doubled; the endowment grew from $350 million to more than $3 billion; the annual operating budget rose from $177 million to more than $650 million; and annual research funding improved from $15 million to more than $70 million. Notre Dame’s most recent capital campaign raised $1.1 billion, far exceeding its goal of $767 million, and is the largest in the history of Catholic higher education.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jenkins_Era:_2005.E2.80.93present">Jenkins Era: 2005–present</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Jenkins Era: 2005–present">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Currently Notre Dame is led by <a href="/wiki/John_I._Jenkins" title="John I. Jenkins">John I. Jenkins</a>, C.S.C., the 17th president of the university.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Jenkins took over the position from Edward &quot;Monk&quot; Malloy, CSC, on July 1, 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> In his inaugural address, Jenkins described his goals of making the university a leader in research that recognizes ethics and building the connection between faith and studies.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>Notre Dame's campus is located in <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame,_Indiana" title="Notre Dame, Indiana">Notre Dame, Indiana</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Unincorporated_area" title="Unincorporated area">unincorporated community</a> in north <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, just north of <a href="/wiki/South_Bend,_Indiana" title="South Bend, Indiana">South Bend</a> and four miles (6&nbsp;km) from the <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> state line.<sup id="cite_ref-About_ND_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About_ND-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Development of the campus began in the spring of 1843, when Father Sorin and some of his congregation built the &quot;Old College,&quot; a building used for <a href="/wiki/Dormitory" title="Dormitory">dormitories</a>, a bakery, and a classroom.</p> 
     <p>A year later, after an architect arrived, a small &quot;Main Building&quot; was built allowing for the launch of the college.<sup id="cite_ref-Hope_4_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hope_4-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Today the campus lies on 1,250 acres (5.1&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) just south of the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Toll_Road" title="Indiana Toll Road">Indiana Toll Road</a> and includes 138 buildings located on <a href="/wiki/Quadrangle_(architecture)" title="Quadrangle (architecture)">quads</a> throughout the campus.<sup id="cite_ref-Campus_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campus-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_God_Quad.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_God_Quad.JPG/170px-University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_God_Quad.JPG" width="170" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_God_Quad.JPG/255px-University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_God_Quad.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_God_Quad.JPG/340px-University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_God_Quad.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_God_Quad.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Historic Washington Hall on the Main Quadrangle, popularly termed the God Quad.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>A number of the buildings that Father Sorin built still stand on the campus, while others have been replaced. The Old College building has become one of two <a href="/wiki/Seminary" title="Seminary">seminaries</a> on campus run by the <a href="/wiki/Congregation_of_Holy_Cross" title="Congregation of Holy Cross">Congregation of Holy Cross</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> The current <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_the_Sacred_Heart,_Notre_Dame" title="Basilica of the Sacred Heart, Notre Dame">Basilica of the Sacred Heart</a> is located on the spot of Sorin's original church, which became too small for the growing college and the Main Building, after a fire destroyed parts of it, has become home to Notre Dame's administration. There are two lakes located on campus, and near the lakes is the Grotto to <a href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Lourdes" title="Our Lady of Lourdes">Our Lady of Lourdes</a>, which was built in 1896 as a replica of the <a href="/wiki/Sanctuary_of_Our_Lady_of_Lourdes" title="Sanctuary of Our Lady of Lourdes">original</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lourdes" title="Lourdes">Lourdes</a>, France.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_sustainability">Environmental sustainability</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Environmental sustainability">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The University of Notre Dame has made being a sustainability leader an integral part of their mission, creating the Office of Sustainability in 2008 to achieve a number of goals in the areas of power generation, design and construction, waste reduction, procurement, food services, transportation, and water.<sup id="cite_ref-About_the_Office.2F.2FOffice_of_Sustainability.2F.2FUniversity_of_Notre_Dame_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About_the_Office.2F.2FOffice_of_Sustainability.2F.2FUniversity_of_Notre_Dame-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup>As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> four building construction projects were pursuing LEED Certified status and three were pursuing LEED Silver.<sup id="cite_ref-Food_Services.2F.2FOffice_of_Sustainability.2F.2FUniversity_of_Notre_Dame_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Food_Services.2F.2FOffice_of_Sustainability.2F.2FUniversity_of_Notre_Dame-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Notre Dame's dining services sources 40% of its food locally and offers sustainably-caught seafood as well as many organic, fair-trade, and vegan options.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Fall_on_%22God_Quad%22_that_Connects_North_Quad_and_South_Quad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Fall_on_%22God_Quad%22_that_Connects_North_Quad_and_South_Quad.jpg/170px-Fall_on_%22God_Quad%22_that_Connects_North_Quad_and_South_Quad.jpg" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Fall_on_%22God_Quad%22_that_Connects_North_Quad_and_South_Quad.jpg/255px-Fall_on_%22God_Quad%22_that_Connects_North_Quad_and_South_Quad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Fall_on_%22God_Quad%22_that_Connects_North_Quad_and_South_Quad.jpg/340px-Fall_on_%22God_Quad%22_that_Connects_North_Quad_and_South_Quad.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Fall_on_%22God_Quad%22_that_Connects_North_Quad_and_South_Quad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Main Quad/God Quad in Autumn
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><sup id="cite_ref-Design_.26_Construction.2F.2FOffice_of_Sustainability.2F.2FUniversity_of_Notre_Dame_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Design_.26_Construction.2F.2FOffice_of_Sustainability.2F.2FUniversity_of_Notre_Dame-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> On the Sustainable Endowments Institute's College Sustainability Report Card 2010, University of Notre Dame received a &quot;B&quot; grade.<sup id="cite_ref-Sustainable_Endowments_Institute_Report_Card_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sustainable_Endowments_Institute_Report_Card-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> The university also houses the Kroc Institute for International Peace Studies. Father Gustavo Gutierrez, the founder of Liberation Theology is a current faculty member.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_buildings">New buildings</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: New buildings">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The university continues to expand and add new buildings each year. Since 2004, many buildings have been built—the most prominent being the DeBartolo Performing Arts Center,<sup id="cite_ref-DPAC_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DPAC-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> the Guglielmino Complex,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Jordan Hall of Science.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, a new male residence hall, Duncan Hall, began construction on March 8, 2007,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> and began accepting residents for the Fall 2008 semester. Ryan Hall has recently finished construction and began housing undergraduate women in the fall of 2009. A new engineering building, Stinson-Remick Hall, a new combination Center for Social Concerns/Institute for Church Life building, Geddes Hall, and a law school addition have recently been completed.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally the new hockey arena opened in the fall of 2011. The Stayer Center for Executive Education, which houses the Mendoza College of Business Executive Education Department opened in March 2013 just South of the Mendoza College of Business building.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="LaFortune_Student_Center">LaFortune Student Center</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: LaFortune Student Center">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The LaFortune Student Center, commonly known as &quot;LaFortune&quot; or &quot;LaFun,&quot; is a 4-story building of 83,000 square feet<sup id="cite_ref-acui.org_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acui.org-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> that provides the Notre Dame community with a meeting place for social, recreational, cultural, and educational activities. The building was constructed in 1883 as a science building but was converted to a student center during the 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-Lafortune_Student_Center_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lafortune_Student_Center-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> LaFortune employs 35 part-time student staff, 29 full-time non-student staff, and has an <a href="/wiki/Budget" title="Budget">annual budget</a> of $1.2&nbsp;million.<sup id="cite_ref-acui.org_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acui.org-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Many businesses, services, and Divisions of Student affairs<sup id="cite_ref-Lafortune_Student_Center_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lafortune_Student_Center-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> are found within. The building also houses restaurants from national restaurant chains.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The building is named for Joseph LaFortune, an oil executive from <a href="/wiki/Tulsa,_Oklahoma" title="Tulsa, Oklahoma">Tulsa, Oklahoma</a>. Joseph LaFortune was the father of Tulsa former mayor <a href="/wiki/Robert_J._LaFortune" title="Robert J. LaFortune">Robert J. LaFortune</a> and the grandfather of former Tulsa mayor <a href="/wiki/Bill_LaFortune" title="Bill LaFortune">Bill LaFortune</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legends_of_Notre_Dame">Legends of Notre Dame</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Legends of Notre Dame">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Legends_of_Notre_Dame" title="Legends of Notre Dame">Legends of Notre Dame</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Legends of Notre Dame (commonly referred to as Legends) is a <a href="/wiki/Music_venue" title="Music venue">music venue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_house" title="Public house" class="mw-redirect">public house</a>, and restaurant located on the campus of the University of Notre Dame, just 100 yards south of <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_Stadium" title="Notre Dame Stadium">Notre Dame Stadium</a>. The former Alumni Senior Club<sup id="cite_ref-SAO_Website_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SAO_Website-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> opened its doors the first weekend in September 2003<sup id="cite_ref-Legends_Observer_Article_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Legends_Observer_Article-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> after a $3.5&nbsp;million renovation and transformed into the all-ages student hang-out that currently exists. Legends is made up of two parts: The Restaurant and Alehouse and the nightclub.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="London_Centre">London Centre</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: London Centre">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The university has had a presence in London, England since 1968. Since 1998, its London Centre has been based in the former <a href="/wiki/United_University_Club" title="United University Club">United University Club</a> at 1, Suffolk Street in Trafalgar Square. The Center enables the Colleges of Arts &amp; Letters, Business Administration, Science, Engineering and the Law School to develop their own programs in London, as well as hosting conferences and symposia.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization_and_administration">Organization and administration</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Organization and administration">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>Since 1967 Notre Dame has been governed by a Board of Trustees, and not directly by the leadership of Holy Cross. The university is governed by two groups, the Board of Fellows and the <a href="/wiki/Trustee" title="Trustee">Board of Trustees</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The Fellows of the University are a group of six Holy Cross religious and six lay members who have final say over the operation of the university. The fellows vote on potential trustees and sign off on all major decisions by that body. The trustees select the president from the United States Province of the Congregation of Holy Cross. In addition to the president, these groups help to maintain the <a href="/wiki/Bylaw" title="Bylaw" class="mw-redirect">bylaws</a> and elect other officers of the university.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Finally, the <a href="/wiki/Provost_(education)" title="Provost (education)">provost</a> of the university, as of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Thomas Burish, works under the president to oversee many of the academic activities and functions of the university.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>As of fall 2006, Notre Dame had 11,603 students and employed 1241 full-time faculty members and another 166 part-time members to give a student/faculty ratio of 13:1.<sup id="cite_ref-Faculty_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faculty-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colleges">Colleges</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Colleges">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_College_of_Arts_and_Letters" title="Notre Dame College of Arts and Letters">College of Arts and Letters</a> was established as the university's first college in 1842 with the first degrees given in 1849.<sup id="cite_ref-Hope_5_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hope_5-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> The university's first academic curriculum was modeled after the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Ratio_Studiorum" title="Ratio Studiorum">Ratio Studiorum</a> from <a href="/wiki/Saint_Louis_University" title="Saint Louis University">Saint Louis University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Today the college, housed in O'Shaughnessy Hall,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> includes 20 departments in the areas of fine arts, humanities, and social sciences, and awards Bachelor of Arts (B.A.) degrees in 33 majors, making it the largest of the university's colleges. There are around 2,500 undergraduates and 750 graduates enrolled in the college.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The College of Science was established at the university in 1865 by president Father Patrick Dillon. Dillon's scientific courses were six years of work, including higher-level mathematics courses.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Today the college, housed in the newly-built Jordan Hall of Science,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_Hesburgh_Library.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_Hesburgh_Library.JPG/220px-University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_Hesburgh_Library.JPG" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_Hesburgh_Library.JPG/330px-University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_Hesburgh_Library.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_Hesburgh_Library.JPG/440px-University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_Hesburgh_Library.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Notre_Dame%27s_Hesburgh_Library.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Hesburgh Library, which is the center of the campus' intellectual life.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>includes over 1,200 undergraduates in six departments of study – biology, chemistry, mathematics, physics, pre-professional studies, and applied and computational mathematics and statistics (ACMS) – each awarding Bachelor of Science (B.S.) degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> According to university statistics, its science pre-professional program has one of the highest acceptance rates to medical school of any university in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_School_of_Architecture" title="Notre Dame School of Architecture">School of Architecture</a> was established in 1899,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> although degrees in architecture were first awarded by the university in 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Today the school, housed in Bond Hall,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> offers a five-year undergraduate program leading to the Bachelor of Architecture degree. One year of study is completed in Rome by all students enrolled in the school.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The College of Engineering was established in 1920,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> however, early courses in civil and mechanical engineering were a part of the College of Science since the 1870s.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Today the college, housed in the Fitzpatrick, Cushing, and Stinson-Remick Halls of Engineering,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> includes five departments of study – aerospace and mechanical engineering, chemical and biomolecular engineering, civil engineering and geological sciences, computer science and engineering, and electrical engineering – with eight B.S. degrees offered. Additionally, the college offers five-year dual degree programs with the Colleges of Arts and Letters and of Business awarding additional B.A. and <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Business_Administration" title="Master of Business Administration">Master of Business Administration</a> (MBA) degrees, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Mendoza_College_of_Business" title="Mendoza College of Business">Mendoza College of Business</a> was established by Father <a href="/wiki/John_Francis_O%27Hara" title="John Francis O'Hara">John Francis O'Hara</a> in 1921, although a foreign commerce program was launched in 1917.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> Today the college offers degrees in accountancy, finance, management, and marketing and enrolls over 1,600 students.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> In the 2010 Bloomberg/Businessweek Undergraduate Business School Rankings, The Mendoza College of Business was ranked as the top overall school.</p> 
     <p>All of Notre Dame's undergraduate students are a part of one of the five undergraduate colleges at the school or are in the First Year of Studies program. The First Year of Studies program was established in 1962 to guide incoming <a href="/wiki/First_year" title="First year" class="mw-redirect">freshmen</a> in their first year at the school before they have declared a major. Each student is given an academic advisor from the program who helps them to choose classes that give them exposure to any major in which they are interested.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> The program also includes a Learning Resource Center which provides time management, collaborative learning, and subject tutoring.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> This program has been recognized previously, by <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i>, as outstanding.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Graduate_and_professional_schools">Graduate and professional schools</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Graduate and professional schools">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The university first offered graduate degrees, in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Arts" title="Master of Arts">Master of Arts</a> (MA), in the 1854–1855 academic year. The program expanded to include <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Laws" title="Master of Laws">Master of Laws</a> (LL.M.) and Master of Civil Engineering in its early stages of growth, before a formal graduate school education was developed with a <a href="/wiki/Dissertation" title="Dissertation" class="mw-redirect">thesis</a> not required to receive the degrees. This changed in 1924 with formal requirements developed for graduate degrees, including offering Doctorate (PhD) degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Today each of the five colleges offer graduate education. Most of the departments from the College of Arts and Letters offer PhD programs, while a professional <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Divinity" title="Master of Divinity">Master of Divinity</a> (M.Div.) program also exists. All of the departments in the College of Science offer PhD programs, except for the Department of Pre-Professional Studies. The School of Architecture offers a <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Architecture" title="Master of Architecture">Master of Architecture</a>, while each of the departments of the College of Engineering offer PhD programs. The College of Business offers multiple professional programs including MBA and <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Accountancy" title="Master of Accountancy">Master of Science in Accountancy</a> programs. It also operates facilities in Chicago and <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">Cincinnati</a> for its executive MBA program.<sup id="cite_ref-Grad_Degrees_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grad_Degrees-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, the Alliance for Catholic Education program<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> offers a <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Education" title="Master of Education">Master of Education</a> program where students study at the university during the summer and teach in Catholic elementary schools, <a href="/wiki/Middle_school" title="Middle school">middle schools</a>, and high schools across the Southern United States for two school years.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In addition to the programs offered by each of the colleges, the <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_Law_School" title="Notre Dame Law School">Notre Dame Law School</a> offers a professional program for students. Established in 1869, Notre Dame was the first Catholic university in the United States to have a <a href="/wiki/Law_school_in_the_United_States" title="Law school in the United States">law program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Law_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Today the program has consistently <a href="/wiki/Law_school_rankings_in_the_United_States" title="Law school rankings in the United States">ranked</a> among the top law schools in the nation according to <i>US News and World Report</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-NDLaw_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDLaw-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> The Law School grants the professional <a href="/wiki/Juris_Doctor" title="Juris Doctor">Juris Doctor</a> degree as well as the graduate LL.M. and <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Juridical_Science" title="Doctor of Juridical Science">Doctor of Juridical Science</a> degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-Grad_Degrees_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grad_Degrees-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Currently, the law school is experiencing an expansion that will double the size of its learning space and is expected to be in operation for the Spring 2009 semester. It is the only accredited American law school to offer a full year of study abroad in London.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>The Joan B. Kroc Institute for International Peace Studies at the University of Notre Dame is dedicated to research, education and outreach on the causes of violent conflict and the conditions for sustainable peace. It offers PhD, Master's, and undergraduate degrees in peace studies. It was founded in 1986 through the donations of <a href="/wiki/Joan_B._Kroc" title="Joan B. Kroc" class="mw-redirect">Joan B. Kroc</a>, the widow of <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald's">McDonald's</a> owner <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kroc" title="Ray Kroc">Ray Kroc</a>. The institute was inspired by the vision of Rev. <a href="/wiki/Theodore_M._Hesburgh" title="Theodore M. Hesburgh" class="mw-redirect">Theodore M. Hesburgh</a> CSC, President Emeritus of the University of Notre Dame. The institute has contributed to international policy discussions about peace building practices.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Though Notre Dame does not have a medical school of its own, it hosts a regional campus of the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_University_School_of_Medicine" title="Indiana University School of Medicine">Indiana University School of Medicine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> where Indiana University medical students may spend the first two years of their medical education before transferring to the main medical campus at <a href="/wiki/Indiana_University_%E2%80%93_Purdue_University_Indianapolis" title="Indiana University – Purdue University Indianapolis">IUPUI</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libraries">Libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Libraries">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Notre_Dame_Law_Library.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/University_of_Notre_Dame_Law_Library.JPG/220px-University_of_Notre_Dame_Law_Library.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/University_of_Notre_Dame_Law_Library.JPG/330px-University_of_Notre_Dame_Law_Library.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/University_of_Notre_Dame_Law_Library.JPG/440px-University_of_Notre_Dame_Law_Library.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Notre_Dame_Law_Library.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The interior of the Kresge Law Library at the 
        <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_Law_School" title="Notre Dame Law School">Notre Dame Law School</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The library system of the university is divided between the main library and each of the colleges and schools. The main building is the 14-story Theodore M. Hesburgh Library, completed in 1963, which is the third building to house the main collection of books.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> The front of the library is adorned with the Word of Life <a href="/wiki/Mural" title="Mural">mural</a> designed by artist <a href="/wiki/Millard_Sheets" title="Millard Sheets">Millard Sheets</a>. This mural is popularly known as &quot;<a href="/wiki/Word_of_Life_mural" title="Word of Life mural" class="mw-redirect">Touchdown Jesus</a>&quot; because of its proximity to <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_Stadium" title="Notre Dame Stadium">Notre Dame Stadium</a> and Jesus' arms appearing to make the signal for a <a href="/wiki/Touchdown" title="Touchdown">touchdown</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> Another piece of artwork associated with the Library is the statue of Moses by Joseph Turkalj. This statue, popularly known as &quot;First Down Moses&quot; because of the manner in which his right arm is outstretched with his right index finger in the air, is at a side entrance to the building.</p> 
     <p>The library system also includes branch libraries for Architecture, Chemistry &amp; Physics, Engineering, Law, the Life Sciences, and Mathematics as well as information centers in the Mendoza College of Business, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kellogg_Institute_for_International_Studies&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kellogg Institute for International Studies (page does not exist)">Kellogg Institute for International Studies</a>, the Joan B. Kroc Institute for International Peace Studies, and a slide library in O'Shaughnessy Hall.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> The library system holds over three million volumes, was the single largest university library in the world upon its completion,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> and remains one of the 100 largest libraries in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rankings">Rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Notre_Dame&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Rankings">edit</a>]</span></h3>
